Connecticut Weekly Desk; SECT14CN
Middlesex Is Highest In a Measure of Income
246 words
4 December 2005
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
2
English
Copyright 2005 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

In 2003, Middlesex County residents had a median household income of $61,770, the highest in Connecticut, according to United States Census Bureau data released on Tuesday.

Households in Tolland County had the second-highest median income, $61,290, while Fairfield County was third at $60,881.

Edward Deak, an economics professor at Fairfield University, said he was surprised that Fairfield County was not first. ''My take is that it's almost exclusively the fact of lower capital gains,'' he said.

The median household incomes for the other Connecticut counties in 2003 were Litchfield, $57,691; Hartford, $51,622; New London, $51,457; New Haven, $50,723; and Windham, $45,929.

The state's median household income, $56,409, was the highest in the country in 2003. New Jersey's was next, at $56,356. The national median was $43,318.

Dr. Deak attributed the state's first-place finish for 2003 to an improving stock market, but pointed out that in 2000, when the market was stronger, the state's median household income was much higher, and first in the nation.

Dr. Deak also warned of the downside of a high median household income. ''The high income reflects high wages, which is good for the people who are working,'' he said. ''But it also makes it difficult for firms to locate and expand here because it is expensive to do business.''

OFF THE CHARTS
Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
Remorseful or Not, Buyers Start to Return to Japanese Real Estate
By Floyd Norris
658 words
3 December 2005
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
3
English
Copyright 2005 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

IN the United States, there is talk about real estate bubbles. In Japan, there is knowledge, and plenty of it, about what happens after one pops.

Figures on housing starts released this week in Tokyo provided evidence that the market finally has gotten over the long and painful decline, and that the Japanese are more willing to buy new homes after avoiding them as prices fell for many years. In the 12 months through October, construction began on 1.22 million homes, nearly 8 percent more than at the low point reached in early 2003.

At the same time, there were limited signs that the growth in the housing market in the United States was leveling off, if not starting to fall. Sales of existing homes came in lower than expected, although new-home sales continue to set records. The number of housing starts in October was a little below the level of a year earlier, and mortgage applications were down.

The ingrained beliefs in consumers in the two countries could not be more different. In the United States, houses have been an excellent investment for years, with property values rising rapidly in many markets. The conventional wisdom was that anyone who waited to buy was foolish.

In Japan, on the other hand, the greatest real estate boom ever seen had, by the late 1980's, led to calculations that the grounds of the Imperial Palace in Tokyo were worth more than the state of California.

''That was followed by 15 years of falling prices,'' said Richard Jerram, an analyst for Macquarie Research who is based in Tokyo. In Central Tokyo, he said, apartment prices probably fell 70 percent to 80 percent from top to bottom. Even with rock-bottom interest rates, many thought it foolish to buy a home that was virtually certain to lose value.

Even now, the prices would not seem cheap to many Americans. Mr. Jerram said a typical Tokyo apartment of 70 square meters, or about 750 square feet, now costs about 40 million yen, or around $330,000. That is affordable to many, given that fixed-rate mortgages are available at 2 percent interest rates.

But those who bought in the past came to regret the decision. ''You could have paid $1.5 million a few years ago,'' Mr. Jerram said.

It appears that home prices hit bottom about two years ago, and since then housing starts have gradually revived.

Over all, Japanese housing starts remain below the levels of 1996, when a planned change in tax law made it seem wise to invest in housing before the law changed. But in the crucial area of housing starts intended for purchase by residents, the number has climbed above 1996 levels.

Among such housing units, there is also a trend toward what the Japanese call collective housing, the equivalent of condominiums in the United States. During the bubble, many who worked in Tokyo were willing to endure two-hour rail commutes by rail to find affordable housing. Now, with prices down, some of those people appear to be buying new apartments in the city.

Japan and the United States are half a world apart, geographically. In terms of long-term housing cycles, they may be even more distant.

Chart: ''As U.S. Boom Slows, Is One Starting in Japan?''

In contrast with the United States, the annual rate of total housing starts in Japan has not recovered since the real estate bubble there burst in 1990 . . .

. . . but more nonrental housing is being built in Japan, and the number of units is likely to rise above 1996 levels this year.

Graph shows the nonrental housing units built in Japan for houses and apartments.

Graph shows change in housing starts since 1989 for the U.S. and Japan.

(Sources by Haver Analytics, Macquarie Research)

Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
Home Sales Fall 2.7%, Suggesting a Drag on '06 Economy
By VIKAS BAJAJ
668 words
29 November 2005
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
4
English
Copyright 2005 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

Sales of existing homes fell 2.7 percent last month and the inventory of houses and condominiums on the market rose to the highest monthly level in more than two years, an industry trade group reported yesterday.

The National Association of Realtors said home sales fell to an annual pace of 7.09 million units in October, down from 7.29 million in September, which was the second-highest level ever.

There were 2.9 million single-family houses, condominiums and co-ops for sale in October, enough to keep the market supplied for 4.9 months, up from 4.6 months in September and the most since June 2003, when there was a 5-month supply.

Economists surveyed by Bloomberg News had expected sales to fall to an annual pace of 7.2 million.

The association estimated that sales would have fallen by an even-greater 3.2 percent in October, had it not been for a surge in activity in cities like Baton Rouge, La., and Jackson, Miss., where evacuees from New Orleans and Mississippi coastal areas have bought homes since Hurricane Katrina struck at the end of August. Sales in the South fell 1.8 percent over all, to an annual pace of 2.8 million units.

Existing-home sales reflect the closing of transactions that were entered into a month or more before.

Still, in an indication that demand for homes remains relatively strong, the median sales price -- half the homes sold for more and half for less -- rose to $218,000 last month from $213,000 in September and was 16.6 percent higher than a year earlier.

''The evidence is consistent with a slowing housing market but one that is still very strong,'' said Patrick Newport, an economist at the research firm Global Insight.

But even a moderate slowdown will have a noticeable impact on economic growth, Mr. Newport indicated, because the rise in home values and the boom in mortgages has helped fuel consumer spending. ''There is no way around it,'' he said. ''The housing market is going to be a drag on the economy next year.''

The latest data reinforced other recent measures of the housing market, like home construction, new-home prices and mortgage applications. Economists and real estate industry officials link the slowing to a rise in interest rates and, in some hot markets, worries that prices have risen too fast in a run of speculation.

The report also appeared to confirm anecdotes from real estate agents and others that houses were taking longer to sell, which suggests a decline in sales and prices in coming months, analysts said.

The inventory increase in condominiums and co-ops -- a 5.5-month supply in October, up from 5.1 months in September -- has been particularly sharp in recent months. There was a 4.8-month supply of single-family houses, up from 4.5 months.

In a note to clients, Ian Shepherdson, chief United States economist at High Frequency Economics, wrote: ''There are now 14.4 percent more homes for sale than a year ago, while actual sales are up just 3.3 percent. With mortgage demand slipping a bit and supply rising, price gains cannot continue at their current pace.''

Home sales were weakest in the Northeast, where they dropped 7.4 percent, and strongest in the West, where they were down 1.2 percent. Sales in the Midwest fell 1.9 percent.

The Realtors' association characterized the sales drop as a healthy cooling of a heated market. ''Housing activity has peaked and is coming down a bit,'' David Lereah, the group's chief economist, said in a statement, ''and we expect further cooling in the coming months.''

The average interest rate on 30-year mortgages was 6.28 percent last week, up from 5.77 percent at the start of the year, according to Freddie Mac, the mortgage reseller.

BIG DEAL
Real Estate Desk; SECT11
When a Sale Isn't a Sale
By WILLIAM NEUMAN
1543 words
27 November 2005
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
2
English
Copyright 2005 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

YOU make an offer on a condo, sign a contract, put together a sizable down payment and look forward to closing. Maybe you even start picking out furniture. People do it every day in Manhattan, but in recent months at the Essex House, the Central Park South mainstay that is both hotel and condominium, a large dose of uncertainty has been injected into that seemingly straightforward transaction.

At least three times in the last 12 months, buyers expecting to close on apartments at the Essex House have seen the units taken away from them by the building's sponsor, which took the unusual step of exercising a right of first refusal to pre-empt the transactions.

Almost all New York condos reserve a right of first refusal for the condominium board, which allows the board to stop a sale if it agrees to buy the apartment for the same amount. But it is rare for boards to do so, largely because few have the cash or the willingness to borrow the money.

But the bylaws of the Essex House condominium are unusual because they establish two rights of first refusal: one for the building's board and the other for the condominium sponsor. And in the case of the Essex House, a succession of companies that have held the role of sponsor have been willing to spend large amounts of money to get their hands on certain apartments. The sponsor currently owns seven condo units in the building. But city records show that since a portion of the Essex House was converted to condos in the 1970's, the sponsor has bought and sold several other units.

The Essex House was acquired for $431 million in September by a company controlled by the crown prince of Dubai, Sheik Mohammed bin Rashid al-Maktoum, and the new owner has shown it is willing to use the sponsor's right of first refusal to buy up units in the building.

A condo buying binge of sorts was begun earlier this year by the previous sponsor, Strategic Hotel Capital, which used the sponsor's right of first refusal to buy Unit 2001 for $1.4 million on Feb. 10, according to a deed filed with the city. Strategic Hotel did the same 15 days later with Unit 3007, paying $1.05 million, records show. In April, it moved to intervene in another sale, saying it planned to take over the contract for Units 901 and 962, which had been combined into a single two-bedroom apartment and had gone to contract in March for $2.365 million.

But this time the buyer went to court, filing a lawsuit against the seller that seeks to block the transfer of the apartment to the sponsor. The buyer was Gerald R. Benjamin, a managing director of Casas, Benjamin & White, a financial advisory firm that specializes in mergers and acquisitions. Lawyers for Mr. Benjamin, who is based in Atlanta and who had made a down payment on the apartment of $236,500, filed the lawsuit in State Supreme Court in Manhattan on June 22. The seller of the apartment, a company called DTF Trucking, got the suit moved to federal court in August.

The Essex House was sold on Sept. 8, but the new owner intends to complete the purchase of the apartment that had been in contract to Mr. Benjamin, according to the sponsor's lawyer, Jeffrey J. Temple. ''When they looked at the apartment, they decided it would be a strategic acquisition,'' Mr. Temple said.

Bruce Blum, an executive vice president of the Oxford Lodging Advisory and Investment Group, the asset manager for the Dubai owners, said a settlement in the case was close, but he would not give details.

Mr. Blum said the spurt of condo buying by the previous sponsor was driven by an expectation that values in the building would rise. Last fall some units were selling for about $1,300 to $1,500 a square foot. Today, he said, they are selling for $2,300 to $2,500 a square foot.

''If you look over the long term, for the most part the sponsor does not buy the units,'' Mr. Blum said. ''Given this recent spike in value in the last 18 months, it turned out to be a very wise decision by the prior sponsor to execute its right of first refusal. It was a bet by the former sponsor that values would increase, and they did.''

He said the Essex House is different from other condos because the sponsor owns the hotel portion of the building, pays about 70 percent of the common charges and controls the condo board. He said the current owners had waived their right of first refusal on about four sales since taking over. ''The right of first refusal is something that came with the acquisition of the property and time will tell whether or not that will be something they will be exercising,'' he said.

Mr. Benjamin refused to discuss the apartment fight. ''The parties are all working on a mutual resolution to the matter,'' he said. Ryan A. Becker, a lawyer for DTF Trucking, said the company would not comment.

But Mr. Benjamin's real estate broker, Monica Dalton, said that the liberal use of the right of first refusal at the Essex House undermines the faith of buyers and that brokers should warn their clients that they might not be able to close on an apartment there. After Strategic Hotel intervened in Mr. Benjamin's purchase, she wrote a letter to condo owners in the building warning that the practice could harm values in the building.

''It's terrible and it's stupid because they're just going to knock themselves right out of the market,'' Ms. Dalton said. ''Knowing this, would you take a client over there?'' Ms. Dalton said that her commission on the sale comes out of the purchase price, so she will get paid even if the sponsor buys, but that she is upset over what her client has gone through.

Cecilia Serrano, a managing director of Warburg Realty Partnership, said that she has sold about 40 apartments in the Essex House and that three of them over the years were taken by the sponsor. The most recent was earlier this year, when she represented both the seller and the intended buyer of Unit 3007, one of the units that Strategic Hotel snapped up in February.

She said the buyer, who was from Mexico, was very upset. The buyer, she said, had spent money traveling to New York to choose the apartment and had paid legal fees related to the expected sale. ''I felt very bad for the buyer,'' Ms. Serrano said, ''but there was absolutely nothing we could do.''

J. Gregory Saver was the lawyer for the seller of Unit 2001, the other apartment Strategic Hotel bought in February. He said he had seen a right of first refusal purchase in a condo only twice in his career -- both times in the Essex House. ''Most condominiums in New York City simply don't have the funds to buy these things,'' Mr. Saver said. But he said that at the Essex House, the sponsor, as a large corporation, has had no difficulty coming up with the purchase price. When he asked why the sponsor wanted Unit 2001, he said, ''They told me they planned to hold it for short-term rentals and they thought it was a good price.''

The buyer who was shut out of that sale was Wallace Turner, a broker with Sotheby's International Realty in Palm Beach, Fla. Mr. Turner, who wanted the apartment as a pied-a-terre, went to contract on the two-bedroom unit in late November 2004 and had expected to close in early January. He and his wife, Betsy, hired a decorator and spent a substantial amount of money buying furniture and antiques to decorate it. Then, on Christmas Eve, he got a call from his lawyer, telling him the deal was off because the sponsor had stepped in to take the apartment. Mr. Turner said he was ''devastated'' by the news.

''Listen, if we're going to spend all kinds of money on designing the apartment and ordering things, we fully expected to get that apartment,'' he said.

The Turners were able to return many of the things they had bought for the apartment, and they canceled orders for others. They have since recovered from their disappointment and gotten back to their New York apartment hunt. Last Monday, they were back in the city, looking at another apartment in -- of all places -- the Essex House. The unit was not right for them, Mr. Turner said, but he is still drawn to the building, with its park views, and he said he would consider trying to buy there again. ''Would we go through all this again?'' he said. ''Yeah, we probably would, but obviously with opened eyes.''

Photos: Crown prince of Dubai, Sheik Mohammed bin Rashid al-Maktoum.; At the Essex House, buyers with contracts lost out to the sponsor. (Photographs by European Pressphoto Agency)

ECONOMIC VIEW
Money and Business/Financial Desk; SECT3
As the McMansions Go, So Goes Job Growth
By DANIEL GROSS
776 words
20 November 2005
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
4
English
Copyright 2005 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

THERE'S a growing consensus that the housing market is cooling off. This month, Toll Brothers, the luxury home builder, warned that sales of McMansions were falling, and its highflying stock fell to earth. Last Thursday, the Commerce Department reported that housing starts, a reliable gauge of present activity, fell 5.6 percent in October from year-ago levels, while building permits, a reliable gauge of future activity, fell 6.7 percent.

These data points are potentially worrisome, and not only for the legions of real estate brokers and Sheetrock layers toiling in offices and job sites across the country. In recent years, economists from Alan Greenspan on down have been discussing the way rising home prices and the growth of home-equity borrowing have fueled consumer spending, the piston that drives the country's economic engine. But in recent years, housing, real estate and the related industries have become a huge factor in another crucial economic area: employment growth.

After the brief and shallow recession of 2001, the resilient United States economy stubbornly failed to create payroll jobs at the rate of past recoveries. Economists and politicians blamed factors and trends like outsourcing, global trade, high benefit costs and productivity growth. But amid the gloom, the real estate sector shouldered the burden of job creation.

Asha Bangalore, an economist at Northern Trust in Chicago, tallied figures from the Bureau of Labor Statistics for sectors like construction, building material and garden supply stores. She found that from November 2001 to October 2005, housing and real estate accounted for a whopping 36 percent of private-sector payroll job growth. ''In four years, 2.3 million private-sector jobs were created in the U.S., and 836,000 were related to the housing sector,'' she said.

Given everything else happening in the corporate world, the housing sector was the ideal place to have an investment boom. Building, selling, decorating and renovating homes is labor intensive. And unlike much investment in the broader manufacturing sector, the money pouring into the housing industry creates demand for American labor. Yes, some housing materials come from overseas. But virtually all the labor associated with housing -- the roofers, the investment bankers who securitize mortgages into bonds, the clerks at Home Depot -- is based in the United States.

As a result of the boom, the economy is more concentrated on housing than ever before. ''Residential investment as a share of gross domestic product is at the highest level in 50 years,'' said Jan Hatzius, senior economist at Goldman, Sachs.

Mark Zandi, chief economist at Economy.com, notes that real-estate-related industries accounted for 9.7 percent of total domestic employment in the second quarter of 2005, up from 9.0 percent in the fourth quarter of 2001. And in areas with the hottest markets, housing plays an even more important role. In California, 13.4 percent of jobs in the second quarter of 2005 were housing-related, versus 12.3 percent in the fourth quarter of 2001. In Las Vegas, the figure rose to 14.6 percent from 12.9 percent; in Panama City, Fla., it rose to 15.4 percent from 11.7 percent.

So what should we expect, now that housing appears to be cooling off?

If the frothy regional markets go flat or if prices simply stop rising at the rates of recent years, there will surely be wide-ranging economic effects on consumer spending and on jobs. ''Housing and the job markets are joined at the hip,'' Mr. Zandi said. ''And if housing cools, so too will hiring and the job market more broadly, particularly in the more juiced-up housing markets.''

If housing prices are flat in 2006 and residential investment falls 5 percent, there could be a direct loss of a few hundred thousand jobs related to real estate, Mr. Hatzius said. And the indirect effects will certainly be larger, Mr. Zandi said: ''Housing is going to go from being a key contributor to the job engine to being a significant drag on job growth.''

But there's some good news. Ms. Bangalore notes that while housing's contribution to job growth has declined in recent months, ''other sectors are picking up the slack.''

Chart: ''Real Estate Jobs''

Real-estate-related jobs as a percentage of total U.S. nonfarm payroll jobs

Graph tracks real-estate-related jobs as a percentage of total U.S. nonfarm payroll jobs from 2001 through 2nd quarter of 2005.

(Source by Economy.com)

Daniel Gross writes the "Moneybox" column for Slate.com.

Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
Home Building Slips to Slowest Pace in Five Months
By VIKAS BAJAJ
606 words
18 November 2005
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
3
English
Copyright 2005 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

New-home construction dropped to its slowest pace in five months and building permits had their biggest monthly drop in almost six years in October, the government reported yesterday, providing more evidence of a housing slowdown.

Although not conclusive, a stream of reports in recent weeks has signaled that the long housing boom is coming off its peak and that slower growth or even a decline might lie ahead.

A monthly report from the Commerce Department yesterday showed that builders started homes at an annual pace of 2.01 million in October, down 5.6 percent from September and down 2.3 percent from October 2004.

Local authorities issued 6.7 percent fewer building permits in the month, for an annual rate of 2.07 million, a decline of 1 percent from October 2004.

''What we are now seeing finally is enough of the housing indicators suggesting that the cooling process has begun to be a believer,'' said David Seiders, chief economist of the National Association of Home Builders.

On Wednesday, the association reported that a monthly index measuring its members' confidence fell in November as builders reported a decline in potential buyers. The group cited mortgage rates, which have risen in the last two months but remain near historical lows, and a decline in consumer confidence.

Earlier this month, Toll Brothers, the nation's biggest maker of luxury homes, reduced its sales forecast for the coming months and year, noting that buyers have become more restrained after Hurricane Katrina.

Mortgage interest rates have been rising steadily for several months. Last week, the average 30-year fixed-rate mortgage carried a 6.36 percent interest rate, up from 5.77 percent at the end of July. Applications for loans for purchases and for refinancing have fallen an average of 2.9 percent in the last four weeks, according to the Mortgage Bankers Association.

Consumer confidence, as measured by leading surveys, fell after Katrina in September and October, but the shopping behavior of Americans appears to provide mixed evidence of that. Retail sales, excluding automobiles and gasoline, have grown briskly in recent months. But real estate agents and others in the industry have reported that homes are staying on the market longer, and that prices are increasing more slowly or even falling in some of the nation's hottest markets.

Reports on other forms of economic activity have looked positive in recent weeks, as they did yesterday. In one, the Federal Reserve said industrial production rose by 0.9 percent in October as more factories, oil refineries and other plants on the Gulf Coast resumed operations. Production fell by 1.5 percent in September because of shutdowns in the region.

The numbers were also bolstered by resumed production at Boeing, which had a machinists' strike in September. Construction and manufacturing were both up 1.4 percent.

''This is further evidence that the economic expansion remains intact,'' Anthony Chan, a senior economist at J. P. Morgan Asset Management, said, noting that gulf recovery accounted for only part of the growth.

The Labor Department reported yesterday that initial claims for unemployment benefits fell to their lowest level in seven months in the week ended Nov. 12 -- to 303,000 from 328,000 the week before. People displaced by the three hurricanes -- Katrina, Rita and Wilma -- added about 19,000 claims to the totals.

Photo: Housing starts in October were down 5.6 percent from September's level as builders cited fewer buyers and rising mortgage interest rates. (Photo by Michael Fein/Bloomberg News)

IN THE REGION/Connecticut
Real Estate Desk; SECT11
Resisting 'Affordable' Homes in Darien
By LISA PREVOST
1211 words
13 November 2005
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
10
English
Copyright 2005 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

DARIEN -- A HUSBAND-AND-WIFE development team is invoking Connecticut's controversial affordable housing law to circumvent restrictions on multifamily housing in an exclusive neighborhood here. Their proposal for 20 units of senior housing on one acre in a section more acclimated toward Wall Street moguls and yachting enthusiasts has riled residents and resurrected calls for repeal of the statute.

The couple, Christopher and Margaret Stefanoni, plan to raze their contemporary home on the Noroton shore of Long Island Sound and build two larger structures designed to resemble a manor house and barn.

Situated on Nearwater Lane, the 20,000-square-foot manor would hold 14 one- and two-bedroom condominiums on three floors; the 9,400-square-foot, three-story barn would house six two-bedroom units. All units would be for buyers 62 and older. ''There's a need for this housing,'' Mrs. Stefanoni said. ''It's huge.''

Six of the one-bedroom condominiums would be sold at below-market rates of $160,000 to $220,000, depending on the buyer's income. To be eligible, a buyer would have to earn 80 percent or less of the state's median income (currently $77,100).

The proposed density level is unheard of in this affluent community of roughly 20,000. The average home in Darien sells for about $1.68 million, and the town's top three taxpayers are country clubs.

Even more unlikely is the site. Zoned for single-family housing, Nearwater Lane is the sole access road to the Noroton Neck peninsula, home of the Noroton Yacht Club and of power brokers like Richard C. Breeden, the former chairman of the Securities and Exchange Commission, and Daniel P. Tully, the former chairman of Merrill Lynch.

The Stefanonis, however, are claiming exemption from local zoning restrictions under Connecticut's Affordable Housing Appeals Law. Under the law, any town in which less than 10 percent of the housing stock meets the state definition of affordable cannot rely on zoning restrictions to deny developments that set aside at least 30 percent of their units as affordable.

An affordable housing developer who is turned away may appeal in court, thereby placing the burden on the town to prove that the project's potential infringement upon the public interest outweighs the need for affordable housing.

''Every year, there are perhaps dozens of bills filed that would either repeal or seriously undermine the law,'' said Jeffrey Freiser, executive director of the nonprofit Connecticut Housing Coalition. But the Legislature has stood by the 1989 law, which, by overcoming ''exclusionary zoning,'' has created an estimated 3,200 units of affordable housing, he said.

Like most Fairfield County suburbs, Darien falls far short of the 10 percent threshold (at 1.8 percent), despite 42 affordable units at a 189-unit complex developed two years ago by AvalonBay Communities. That project's 30-acre site is next to the railroad tracks in the more-densely-developed Noroton Heights.

The prime location of the Stefanoni project has focused attention on Darien's exclusivity, only adding to residents' resentment. At a Planning and Zoning Commission hearing on the proposal earlier this month, one attendee cursed at a television cameraman, suggesting he go cover some other event. As the Stefanonis presented their proposal, the audience of more than 300 people snickered until the chairman asked that the heckling stop. The hearing was continued until Nov. 29 to accommodate the large number of speakers.

As the Stefanonis see it, residents' objections are grounded in fear of what an affordable housing project will bring. ''There's a lot of closed-mindedness about who people like that are and what they'll do to property values,'' Mrs. Stefanoni said.

The young parents of four (with a fifth on the way), the Stefanonis said the homogeneity of Darien produced a ''culture shock'' when they arrived seven years ago from Cambridge, Mass., a much more diverse municipality. Not developers by profession, the couple are circumspect about how they make a living, saying only that they specialize in identifying properties with title problems and removing encumbrances to make them salable as single-family homes.

The Nearwater Manor project came about because providing housing for seniors is ''the right thing to do,'' Mrs. Stefanoni said.

About 60 seniors are on a waiting list for openings at Darien's only low-income elderly housing complex, according to Jo Lorraine Tatum, executive director of the Town Housing Authority. Turnover at the 30-unit rental complex is slow, averaging about three openings a year.

Judith Morrison, Darien's social services director, also senses the need. ''We get lots and lots of calls from people looking for housing for their parents or for themselves because they're downsizing,'' she said.

But the Stefanonis' expressions of altruism are laughable to opponents, who accuse the couple of using the affordable housing law to squeeze a more-profitable high-density project onto an inappropriate site.

An e-mail message circulated before the hearing cautioned critics to avoid making public objections to the affordable and senior aspects of the project, lest they buttress Mr. Stefanoni's public arguments about the town's insensitivity to those who are not wealthy ''and make him more of a martyr.'' Speakers at the hearing cited concerns about traffic, emergency access to the site and the scale of the project.

Most upsetting for some is the possibility that it will set a precedent. If the Stefanonis' project is approved, other developers may quickly follow suit, said Joseph R. Warren, an elected member of the town's Representative Town Meeting, which has no role in deciding this application.

The project ''would do more to destroy the residential character of Darien than anything I have seen in recent years,'' Mr. Warren told the audience. ''It is in Hartford where this has to be addressed.''

Neighbors of the Nearwater Lane site take the project more personally. The senior housing proposal is ''sort of an exclamation point'' to several years of back-and-forth litigation involving the Stefanonis and at least two neighbors over the couple's right to an easement granting access to the water and their extensive cutting of trees, said Bruce Hill, a lawyer for three abutting neighbors.

The Stefanonis' development plan calls for 16-foot pole lights against one neighbor's boundary line, Mr. Hill said. A parking lot is three feet from another neighbor's property line, while six compressors for the central air-conditioning system are just 10 feet from the line. ''It almost seems deliberately to have violated every zoning regulation,'' he said.

Though the Stefanonis acknowledge that they have had legal battles with their neighbors, they insist that allegations of retaliation are no more legitimate than claims that the project will cause traffic congestion.

''It would be a lot easier to just build a McMansion, sell and move to another town,'' Mr. Stefanoni said. ''But Darien has enough McMansions. Darien needs some humility.''

Real Estate Desk; SECT11
More Buyers Are Thinking Small
By PATRICK O'GILFOIL HEALY
1760 words
13 November 2005
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
1
English
Copyright 2005 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

IS the safest bet in New York real estate also the smallest one?

Years ago, studios and one-bedrooms were the dingy wares at the bottom of Manhattan's property bin. Brokers disdained the tiny commissions their cheap prices brought. Common wisdom dictated that small apartments were poor investments.

But recent market reports show that studios and one-bedroom apartments are increasing in price, while the values of larger apartments are not keeping pace.

Studios and one-bedrooms made up 55 percent of all sales from July to September -- the most recent quarter -- compared with a market share of 50 percent last spring. Their average values climbed 11 percent, while prices on larger apartments fell an average of 16 percent.

Market data show that the average studio in Manhattan now costs $428,831, and the average one-bedroom is $687,744. In some neighborhoods, studios are more expensive, per square foot, than the average for all apartments. The most recent median price data was not available for different sizes of apartments.

''I've seen a huge increase in the studios, and it's because of the first-time buyer,'' said Vickey Barron, an agent with Prudential Douglas Elliman. ''A few years ago people would have waited. They would have passed on the studio and pounced on the one-bedroom. Now the one-beds are priced out of their range. They're coming out of the woodwork.''

The shift has surprised some brokers, who remember when small apartments languished on the market.

''When I first started in the business 10 years ago, most brokers wouldn't even handle one-bedrooms,'' said Denice Rich, a broker with the Corcoran Group, because there was too much supply, and movement was slow. ''I was the only one who would take the sale.''

Of course, this sector hasn't become magically inoculated against slow spells and price drops. But real estate brokers and analysts say small apartments are now more resilient. For one, construction in Manhattan is geared toward sprawling luxury and not modest affordability, limiting the number of new one-bedrooms and studios entering the market.

Because they are cheaper to buy and maintain, small apartments also make natural second homes or pieds-a-terre for commuters or suburbanites tiptoeing into the city, according to Jonathan J. Miller, president of the appraisal firm Miller Samuel. And they are often easier to rent, and draw high rents relative to an owner's mortgage burden, making them more profitable investments for buyers looking for rental income.

The buyers buoying this wedge of the market are largely students and young professionals planting a foot in Manhattan, rather than families of four squeezing into a too-small home, brokers say. On the low end of the scale, they are singles looking for the cheapest studio available.

On the higher end, they are buyers who could have afforded a two-bedroom in 2000 but now must settle for a large one-bedroom.

Typical of the young buyers is Laura Cave, 24, who bought a 450-square-foot studio at 20th Street and Seventh Avenue after moving from California to study graduate-level humanities at New York University. As with many younger buyers in the studio market, Ms. Cave's parents financed the purchase, and she pays rent to them. The apartment is in all their names.

Because Ms. Cave plans to stay in New York after she graduates, her family decided that buying a small place would be a smart investment. They wanted an apartment near Gramercy, Murray Hill or Chelsea that could be renovated to increase its value.

Inventory analyses show that about 140 studios a month are arriving on the market this year, about the same rate as last year. Many of those were beyond Ms. Cave's grasp, or below her standards. The cheaper ones -- around $300,000 to $350,000 -- were tiny and shabby, with slanted floors and bad lighting. Other buildings had income requirements or policies against parental buyers.

''We didn't really have a lot of options because our price range was really low,'' Ms. Cave said. ''We were like, anything that wasn't a hovel, we'd look at. It was definitely a shock coming from L.A.''

Eventually, they found the studio on 20th Street, a clean and bright space with a sunken living area, separate kitchen and enough room for a full-sized bed and bulging sofa.

Younger buyers like Ms. Cave are not active only in New York; they make up the fastest-growing segment of the real estate market in the country, according to census data from 2004, the most recent available. The number of homeowners younger than 25 more than doubled over the last decade, to 1.64 million from 807,000.

Ignoring the loft-buying Olsen twins, almost all of these younger buyers have incomes and savings that qualify them for entry-level apartments at entry-level prices, said Avi Voda, a broker with Douglas Elliman.

At the high end of the small apartment market are couples like David and Ann Rosenbach, who live in Tampa, Fla., but fly to New York 10 times a year to visit friends, family and the Carnegie Deli. The Rosenbachs had talked for years about buying a second home in the city before finally snapping up a large one-bedroom on Central Park South last winter.

Ms. Rosenbach, an event planner, and her husband, a radiologist, had always wanted a two-bedroom apartment. But as prices rose, it became more difficult to find anything below their $1 million price limit. So they started looking at one-bedrooms with an eye toward renovation.

''There is something different about a sense of ownership,'' Dr. Rosenbach said. ''It's so different from being a visitor. It's a sense of being a part of the city that I didn't have before.''

When they found a 1,100-square-foot one-bedroom at 128 Central Park South, they pounced. They bought the eighth-floor apartment and soon began construction to add another bedroom. They probably saved tens of thousands of dollars by building their own two-bedroom.

Buyers have always paid a premium for space and rooms in Manhattan, where prices per square foot for the largest apartments are easily double or triple the cost per square foot of a modest one-bedroom. Market data from Brown Harris Stevens show that the average price gap between studios and one-bedroom co-ops is $229,685. The leap from one-bedrooms to two-bedrooms is $693,358.

But those price gaps and disparities are shrinking, market data shows.

Three years ago, prices of classic six apartments were rocketing past smaller homes. Two- and three-bedroom apartments sold for an average of $816 a square foot, while studios and one-bedrooms sold for $524 a square foot -- a $292 difference.

A third-quarter report by Brown Harris Stevens shows that the disparity between these smaller and larger homes has shrunk to $241 a square foot.

In some neighborhoods, studio values have actually outpaced one-bedrooms, according to an analysis by Miller Samuel. On a square-foot basis, studios are more expensive than one-bedrooms in Yorkville, Hell's Kitchen, the Financial District and Turtle Bay, the analysis found.

Near Lincoln Center, studios cost $133 more per square foot than one-bedrooms, according to the Miller Samuel data. Along Riverside Drive and West End Avenue, they are $264 more per square foot. On the Lower East Side, Riverside Drive and in Midtown West, studios cost more per square foot than the average apartment price, which includes prices for two-, three- and four-bedrooms. Only in Chelsea did one-bedrooms cost more per square foot than an average apartment.

Still, not everyone selling small homes is throwing confetti. Denise Rosner, a senior vice president at Bellmarc Realty, said she sees weakness in this sector. She says one-bedrooms are sitting longer on the market, and apartments priced at $725,000 are being discounted and sold for $655,000.

''Lately, the buyers that are out there aren't really a lot to write home about,'' Ms. Rosner said.

But for studio owners like Pam Shiu, 32, a buyer for Ann Taylor, rapid price increases have opened the door for better neighborhoods and larger homes. Seven years after she bought a studio in the Cherokee Apartments building on York Avenue for $72,000, Ms. Shiu decided to cash in. Over the summer, she sold her studio for $290,000 and bought a $435,000 one-bedroom in Park Slope, Brooklyn.

''I wanted to have a grown-up apartment, with cupboard space,'' Ms. Shiu said. ''It was definitely a maturing phase.''

Some buyers said they worried about investing in homes whose value may have peaked. Studio values rose 35 percent over the last year, and one-bedrooms climbed by 27 percent, according to Miller Samuel. But what if that growth slows or stops?

Suzana Grego, 33, chewed over those questions last month before she bought a one-bedroom in the West 80's for $645,000. It was her first home purchase in New York City and she worried about buying at the market's peak.

''I was thinking: 'This is not the time to be doing this. Do I really need to be doing this?' '' said Ms. Grego, a communications director for a nonprofit group. ''The one thing that made me worry less, the one thing that calmed me down is that I think you have to buy a place for the quality of life. Am I going to be happy here? Yes. Did I get what I want? Yes. I think the value is there.''

Westchester Weekly Desk; SECT14WC
A Market Intolerant Of Overpricing
By ELSA BRENNER
915 words
13 November 2005
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
4
English
Copyright 2005 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

CORRECTION APPENDED

A picture caption last Sunday with an article about Westchester real estate misstated the price and the location of an eight-room co-op. The listed price is $949,000, not $900,000; the building is in Yonkers, not Bronxville.

CORRECTED BY THE NEW YORK TIMES Sun Nov 20 2005

AT dinner parties, on train platforms and in the supermarket aisle, as Westchester indulges in its favorite topic -- real estate -- one word is being heard more often than any other.

''There's all this talk about bubble, bubble, bubble,'' said Joe Houlihan, an owner of Houlihan & O'Malley in Bronxville. ''The word has become part of the vernacular.''

But is there a bubble? If so, is it about to burst?

Local real estate professionals maintain resolutely that what we are witnessing is merely a healthy market coming out of an extraordinary growth spurt.

An overinflated market, about to explode? That is the unlikeliest of possibilities, they insist. In fact, the Westchester-Putnam Multiple Listing Service revealed in an October report that house prices have again reached record heights.

Yet for sellers about to list their homes, the numbers in the October report argue against unrealistic optimism in pricing. ''There were signs that the breakneck local market of the past two years had begun to ease to a more sustainable pace,'' was the phrasing it used.

It also found that the double-digit price gains of the last two years had outpaced the financial capacities of many buyers -- even those relying on equity accrued in their present homes for trading up.

''In other words, sellers cannot command excessively high prices,'' said Sona Davidian, a broker and owner of C. S. Mc Clellan in Pelham. ''They have to be realistic and look carefully at what similar properties are actually selling for.''

The best strategy, Ms. Davidian said, is to price a house correctly to begin with -- especially with bidding wars occurring less frequently these days.

Another factor: In early winter during the holiday season, sales velocity slows. A house that goes on the market too late in the fall, or at too high a price, may languish, and properties that become stale cause some buyers to shy away.

Sellers looking to avoid such pitfalls should act now and not expect the windfall profits of previous quarters, agents advise.

At the end of the third quarter of this year, the median sales price of a single-family home hit $711,700 -- 4 percent higher than for the third quarter a year ago.

The median for a condominium soared to $390,000 -- an all-time record, 12 percent higher than it was a year ago. And the median for cooperatives jumped 16 percent in a year, to $185,000.

But when it came to the number of sales, the story was slightly different. The listing service reported that in the third quarter, the number of single-family houses sold was down by 5 percent, to 2,002, and the number of co-ops sold was down by 1 percent, to 620. Condo sales increased by 3 percent.

Also, while dollar volume of sales for single-family homes edged downward more than 1 percent, the dollar volume of sales for condos and co-ops increased by 20 percent and 13 percent, respectively.

Another significant variable in assessing market strength is inventory. For the third quarter of this year it rose, to the concern of some experts. They point out that the supply of for-sale units typically declines from the second to the third quarters, reflecting brisk sales in the spring and summer months. This year, though, the end-of-quarter inventory for all types of for-sale units (single-family houses, co-ops, condos and multifamily homes) was 6,005 -- 21 percent higher than a year ago.

Market analysts are carefully watching a 45 percent year-to-year increase in the supply of condos, to 688 units, from 473 last year.

''It is too early to tell from one quarter's experience whether this was a statistical fluke or a consequence of more units coming online from new construction, true market retraction, or some other anomaly in that sector of the market,'' the listing service said in its report.

Still, third-quarter inventory numbers represent only a small portion of Westchester's total housing stock (more than 200,000 owner-occupied units). And considered from a historical perspective, they were far below the 10,000 units available in the 1980's and early 90's.

''There's still an excess of demand over supply,'' said P. Gil Mercurio, executive director of the listing service.

George M. Stone, a co-owner of Julia B. Fee in Scarsdale, said: ''My advice is that we are in a very healthy market, and it's still a good time to buy and sell.''

''But sellers of 20th-century homes should realize they are competing in a market for the attention of buyers with 21st-century needs,'' added Mr. Stone, whose agency has offices along the Long Island Sound and recently acquired Burbank/Whittemore in Larchmont.

House & Home/Style Desk; SECTF
For Choicest Apartments, Many More Choices
By MOTOKO RICH
1419 words
10 November 2005
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
1
English
Copyright 2005 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

WHEN developers opened new buildings for sale in New York a year ago, brokers jostled for appointments, buyers camped out before open houses and people made decisions to part with thousands of dollars in a matter of minutes. One sales agent famously sold five luxury condos from the back seat of her BMW in a day.

The frenzy has died down, but the buildings keep going up.

With close to 16,000 units being built in Manhattan alone this year and next, and another 23,700 planned, according to Yale Robbins, a real estate publishing company in New York, there is much to choose from, especially at the high end, where a disproportionate amount of building is going on.

The plentiful options mean that buyers can now return to a sales office repeatedly to examine floor plans before writing a deposit check. Where some buildings might have sold three or four apartments in a day, now developers are happy to sell that many in a month. And some buyers even feel bold enough to offer less than the asking price, though few developers are biting.

''I just feel like people are waiting to pull the trigger,'' said Ariana Meyerson, project manager at 225 Fifth Avenue, a 192-unit building with $1 million one-bedroom apartments. ''They are waiting to see where the market goes.''

Some brokers predict that sales will slow more as newer apartments, often with fancier amenities than the building down the street, come on the market. ''I do think that there is going to be product sitting on the market a year from now,'' said Michael Shvo, a broker who markets new developments.

Citywide, developers applied for building permits representing 15,870 new units in the first six months of this year, census figures show. Last year they applied for 25,208 units, the most since 1971. A large proportion of the newest units are being marketed as ''luxury'' apartments, a term that has come to mean anything from $600,000 studios in Brooklyn to $40 million penthouses on Central Park West.

The bounty of choice includes the planned condominiums at the Plaza Hotel and in the Stanhope Hotel on Fifth Avenue near the Metropolitan Museum; chic new apartments designed by the French architect Jean Nouvel at 40 Mercer Street in SoHo; and the Philip Johnson-designed units at the Urban Glass House on Spring Street nearby.

''We do have a healthy amount of developments coming on stream,'' said Kirk Henckels, director of Stribling Private Brokerage in Manhattan. ''And if there is one question mark'' looming about the state of the market, he said, ''that may be it.''

Although condo prices are still increasing, brokers said buyers are being shrewder about what they are willing to pay. Six months ago the luxury market was so heated that ''you could basically put a 3,000-square-foot coal chute on the market and three people would try to buy it,'' said Frederick W. Peters, president of Warburg Realty. Now, he said, buyers were being more selective about location and quality.

Stacey Silverman, 41, has been looking for a two-bedroom apartment in a new development for the past six months. She has yet to put down a deposit. ''I think there is a lot to choose from, and there is more coming on the market,'' she said. ''So I'm taking my time.''

Some developers have not caught on to this new reality. ''Sellers are in denial that the market has shifted,'' said Jonathan Miller, a New York appraiser. ''On projects that appear to have stalled, the developer kept ratcheting up the prices systematically -- which was the norm for the past few years -- until it was priced out of the market.''

Some new developments are still selling briskly, even at the superluxury end of the market. The developers of the condominiums at 15 Central Park West have had no trouble selling 74 units worth $650 million in the two months since the sales office opened.

William Zeckendorf, a partner in the development, said 12 of 16 penthouses, including one at $45 million, were under contract. A prominent Wall Street investor lost out on a duplex penthouse when he tried to buy at a discount and another buyer offered full price, Mr. Zeckendorf added.

But brokers said the scramble at 15 Central Park West, designed by Robert A. M. Stern in the spirit of such grand buildings as the San Remo and the Dakota, was largely a reflection of its irresistible location, lavish amenities and old-world charm.

Alex and Luba Rabey were not even looking to move from their apartment at the Grand Millennium on Broadway when they heard about 15 Central Park West. Mr. Rabey, a 65-year-old consultant, said they were attracted to the building's particular vantage on the park, its gym facilities and its 11-foot ceilings. They have signed a contract to buy a $4.87 million three-bedroom apartment.

Name architects have not necessarily spurred sales at either the Richard Meier tower at 165 Charles Street or Charles Gwathmey's undulating glass building at Astor Place. They are both in downtown locations that some brokers said wealthy buyers do not prefer.

The tower at 165 Charles Street, the third to be designed by Mr. Meier overlooking the West Side Highway, has been on the market for over 18 months. Out of 31 units, 24 have sold, including a $20 million penthouse. One broker who asked not to be named because she did not want to criticize a building where she might bring clients said it should have sold out by now. Izak Senbahar, the developer, said, ''It's a very specialty product that sells at its own speed.''

Despite extensive media coverage and a model apartment sponsored by Esquire magazine, the Related Companies, developer of the Gwathmey building, has sold only 24 of 39 units there. David J. Wine, vice chairman of Related, said he was perfectly happy with those numbers, given what buyers were paying: $1,400 to $2,600 a square foot.

But, he acknowledged, ''buyers, at those numbers, because they have more choices, are being more cautious about where they buy.''

Even at less lofty levels, buyers are hesitating. ''It's taking longer to sell out buildings,'' said Richard Cantor, a principal at Cantor & Pecorella, a marketing firm that is handling 1,500 units in 10 new buildings around Manhattan.

It has taken 10 months to sell 43 of the 79 units at Beacon Tower, a high-rise condominium in the Dumbo neighborhood of Brooklyn, despite the lure of a Zen garden, a gym and high-end kitchens. Steve Rutter, who handles marketing of new developments for Corcoran Group in Brooklyn, said the developer, Leviev Boymelgreen, closed down the sales office for part of the summer because sales were sluggish, and reopened after Labor Day. Only four apartments have sold since then.

So far, nobody is predicting a glut or that prices will collapse. That is partly because today's building boom pales in comparison to the one that took place in the 1980's (developers built 125,623 units in Manhattan from 1985 to 1991, Mr. Miller, the appraiser, said). That building spree exacerbated the effects of the stock market crash and a severe recession, and median prices fell as much as 45 percent.

Since 1999 builders have put up only 13,374 units, about a tenth of what was built in the 1980's.

This time around, Mr. Miller pointed out, developers have focused on the upper third of the market, making it vulnerable to the impulses of one group of buyers.

For the moment, many remain in the game. Ali Akansu, a professor at the New Jersey Institute of Technology, recently bought an $815,000 one-bedroom apartment in Bryant Park Tower, a 94-unit condominium that will sit atop a hotel. He said he figured that it would be a good investment, and that maybe his college-age daughter would move in at some point. But when he was looking, he said, he saw several other projects that he deemed overpriced.

Photos: INVENTORY -- New luxury housing includes a collaboration between Jean Nouvel and Andre Balazs on Mercer Street in SoHo, rendering at far left, and a glass tower at Astor Place designed by Charles Gwathmey. (Photo by Right, Michael Falco for The New York Times)(pg. F4)

Business/Financial Desk; SECTC
Big Builder Sees Slower Home Sales
By VIKAS BAJAJ and DAVID LEONHARDT; Motoko Rich contributed reporting for this article.
1332 words
9 November 2005
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
1
English
Copyright 2005 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

The nation's largest maker of luxury homes, Toll Brothers, said yesterday that soaring home prices appeared to have ended. It was the latest sign that many real estate markets are slowing.

Although the company said it expected to report a record profit for the last 12 months, it predicted that it would sell fewer homes over the next year than it had forecast and would make less money than previously anticipated. High gas prices and the recent hurricanes seem to have rattled consumers, causing some to delay purchases of houses, company executives said. Also, some local governments appear to be holding back on construction permits, to slow new building.

Shares of Toll Brothers fell after yesterday's announcement and ended the day down 14 percent, at $33.91. The news dragged the shares of other home builders lower, and the Bloomberg United States home builders index fell 7 percent yesterday.

''The price increases pre-Katrina were at warp speed, and since Katrina, instead of going up $5,000 or $10,000 every week or two, we have been limited to no price increases or very limited price increases,'' Robert I. Toll, the company's chief executive, said in a conference call yesterday. The number of investors buying condominiums and houses in the hope of turning a quick profit also seems to have plunged, he said in an interview last week. ''The true speculator is gone from the market,'' Mr. Toll said.

Certain markets appear to be losing steam faster than others, Mr. Toll said. Washington, Chicago and Northern California were slowing from high levels of activity, while Boston, Denver and the west coast of Florida were still hot markets for the company. Las Vegas, which has experienced a huge condo building boom, slowed down ''ever so slightly'' in July and appears to be staying at that level, he added.

The company, whose homes sell at an average price of $679,000 and whose typical buyer has an annual family income of more than $100,000, emphasized that it still expected a happy ending for the long housing boom.

Home prices, the company said, are expected to continue rising, but at historical averages and not at the rapid rates that they have recently in the Northeast and California. Since 1964, new-home prices have risen an average of 6 percent a year, according to the Commerce Department. Last year, they rose 13 percent.

Even with its reduced forecast, Toll Brothers expects to sell 7 percent more homes in its current fiscal year, which began Nov. 1, than it did in the previous year. Last year, the company's revenue rose 50 percent, to $5.8 billion, according to preliminary figures released yesterday. The company will disclose detailed financial results and projections for 2006 on Dec. 8.

In many of the nation's hottest real estate markets, houses are taking much longer to sell than they once did, and some agents are talking about a slowdown in attendance at open houses. In September, there was a 4.7-month supply of already- built homes on the market, up from 4.2 months a year ago, according to the National Association of Realtors.

Mr. Toll said last week that in some markets, the company was starting to offer incentives like a luxury kitchen at no extra cost to the buyer.

J. Patrick Lashinsky, a senior vice president at ZipRealty, a brokerage firm with offices in 15 metropolitan areas, said, ''In a lot of our markets, we're seeing inventory levels up 20, 30, 40 percent from a few months ago.'' Some of the biggest increases were in the Phoenix, San Diego and San Francisco areas, he said, and the number of houses for sale had also risen in Atlanta, Boston and Chicago.

For the moment, at least, prices have stopped their steep climb, with some owners reducing asking prices to attract buyers. Average sale prices traditionally drop in autumn, because home purchases slow then, but the decline appears to have been steeper than usual this year.

Between August and September, the median sales price of already-built homes dropped 3.6 percent nationwide, to $212,000. During the same period last year, prices fell 1.6 percent, according to the National Association of Realtors.

The median sales price for new homes fell 5.7 percent, to $215,700, from August to September, according to the Commerce Department. That compares with a 3 percent drop a year ago.

Richard A. Smith, chairman and chief executive of Cendant's real estate business, said he, too, expected a slowing in the housing market next year, but that 2006 would still be one of the strongest years on record for home sales. ''The problem with forecasting this time of the year is, if you look at the seasonality of the business, this is always the slowest time of the year,'' Mr. Smith said.

The slowdown appears to be linked to a steady rise in mortgage rates in recent months. The average rate on 30-year fixed mortgages was 6.31 percent last week, up from 6.15 percent the week before and 5.77 at the start of the year, according to Freddie Mac. Even with the increases, the rates are still below historical averages.

Loan applications for purchases and refinancing fell 4.8 percent in the last week of October and were 15.2 percent lower than the same week last year, the Mortgage Bankers Association said. Loans for purchases fell 6.2 percent for the week and 11.9 percent from a year ago.

Some economists argue that the recent softening might be the beginning of a more severe downturn. Neither income nor population has grown quickly enough to justify the doubling of prices in some areas since 2000, these economists say. They add that rents have risen much less than home prices.

''There is a large psychological element behind current housing prices,'' said Dean Baker, co-director of the Center for Economic Policy Research in Washington. ''People are willing to pay them because there is an expectation that prices will continue to rise. Once people don't have that expectation, things will change.''

Even many analysts who are not so pessimistic say the recent increases cannot continue.

''Home prices have been growing so fast for so long, outstripping income growth for many years,'' said Gene Huang, the chief economist of FedEx, who keeps an eye on home prices as an overall gauge of the economy's health. ''Some kind of adjustment has to happen.''

Per capita disposable income grew 3.6 percent over the last year, according to the Commerce Department.

More than four out of five economic forecasters surveyed by Blue Chip Economic Indicators said they expected existing-home price growth to slow to 5 percent by the end of 2006, from 13 percent over the last 12 months.

''All of a sudden, the ads in the paper show special mortgage deals, special incentives,'' Mr. Toll said last week, referring to new housing developments nationally. ''That's an absolute indicator that the market has softened.''

Mr. Toll also attributed his company's new sales forecast to toughening local regulations, saying governments in many cities were delaying new projects to placate existing residents who want slower growth.

''We are building in pretty well-established territory which means it's also pretty well regulated,'' he said on the conference call. ''We have a heavier-hitting average population, and their desire is 'Not in my backyard, please slow it down.' ''

BIG BUILDER SEES SLOWER HOME SALES

Long Island Weekly Desk; SECT14LI
Altitude Sickness
By KEN BELSON
2017 words
6 November 2005
The New York Times
NYTF
Late Edition - Final
1
English
Copyright 2005 The New York Times Company. All Rights Reserved.

IT has been the unbroken code of Long Island living, at least since Levittown and American suburbia were born in the 1940's: Buy a home, hold on to it and watch its value climb.

With only an occasional pause, the axiom has held true through recession, inflation and expansion, and the red-hot property market of recent years has done little to tarnish it. On paper, at least, homeowners have been rewarded as property values have soared to unprecedented heights.

But this paper prosperity comes at a cost, one that is growing so fast that it is starting to erode the benefits for anyone who is not ready to cash in now and relocate to some less-expensive region.

Over the last decade, home prices have outpaced incomes by such a wide margin that many of Long Island's festering problems -- worsening shortages of rental housing and of lower-priced homes, taxes that are high and still rising, the sometimes prohibitive costs of doing business and providing social services, and the flight of young families -- have become harder to ignore.

Barring a sudden downward spiral in home prices -- which few experts foresee and which would create another set of headaches -- Long Island's leaders have little choice but to confront the politically thorny problems surrounding high prices, which are sapping the region's economic vitality.

Otherwise, Long Island is likely to go on losing businesses and young residents to other regions at an accelerating clip, economists say. Nassau and Suffolk, whose populations are already aging faster than the nation as a whole, will grow even grayer, putting more strain on the region's finances and inhibiting its growth.

''New businesses and young college graduates are discouraged from locating here,'' Thomas R. Suozzi, the Nassau County Executive, said earlier this year. ''And the seniors who moved here when suburbia first began 50 or 60 years ago are now house-rich but income-poor, and are especially hurting.''

A litany of troubling figures supports Mr. Suozzi's claims. Since 1998, housing prices on Long Island have nearly doubled as a percentage of family income, making it difficult not just for low-income families, but also for people with middle-class jobs, to buy a home. The median sale price hit $470,000 this year in Nassau and $385,000 in Suffolk.

The usual way to ease such a strain is for local lawmakers to allow more land to be developed. But Long Island is rapidly running out of available property for new homes; by one count, there are just 67,000 acres of suitable land left.

With land scarce, the only way to increase the housing supply is to allow more living units on each acre. But plans for high-density housing are usually bitterly resisted by suburbanites because of the association with urban problems like poverty and crime.

''There are lingering fears in the suburbs that we're inviting the city in'' if we build high-density housing, said Pearl M. Kamer, the chief economist at the Long Island Association, the Island's largest business organization. ''Most people came from the city, but they like to shut the door when they get here.''

The same goes for rental housing in general, which has been strongly discouraged for years by zoning laws in many Island communities. Long Island has only half as much residential rental space, as a percentage of housing, as the national average; just 19 percent of all housing units on the Island are rentals, and the rents they command are much higher than they would be if the supply matched the national average.

Anyone who already has a home and is not currently looking to move may be tempted to shrug off high prices and short supply as of no immediate concern to them. But the hot housing market is taking a growing toll even on those who are not in the market and have no plans to move.

The most significant way is through property taxes. Local governments rely heavily on them, and the taxes Island homeowners pay are among the highest in the country; they have continued to rise along with home prices, which have grown at double-digit rates in each of the past five years.

Taxes are rising in great measure because the cost of doing anything on Long Island, from repaving a street to hiring a middle-school principal, has been driven up by the high cost of housing and the higher wages that employers, including governments, must pay to attract and keep workers. High house prices also make it much more expensive for municipalities to acquire land they need for public purposes like parks or widened streets, which also feeds back into higher taxes.

People on fixed incomes, like retirees, and working people whose paychecks have not kept pace with their tax bills are being squeezed hard by the rising burden, despite their paper gains in net worth from the appreciation of their houses. ''You can't pay your taxes with the equity in your house,'' said Steve Levy, the Suffolk County executive. ''It helps you only if you sell it and move.''

Higher house prices mean that businesses and governments, schools and hospitals all have a hard time recruiting promising new employees from other regions of the country to live on Long Island. And the employers who do manage to bring young recruits to the Island often find that they lose them again in a few years, when they prepare to start a family, because suitable homes on the Island are out of their reach.

Similarly, many native Long Islanders who are starting careers find that the only way to afford a place to live other than mom and dad's spare room is to leave the region.

According to the Long Island Association, between 2000 and 2003, Nassau and Suffolk suffered a net loss of 26,000 residents between the ages of 25 and 34, a loss five times the national average in proportion to the total population. Among people 35 to 44, the net loss was 13,000 people over the same period.

Some older people are also going because their children are. ''If your children can no longer live here, why not move where your kids are?'' said Nancy Douzinas, president of the Rauch Foundation, which compiles an annual statistical study of Long Island life. ''People are leaving in tandem.''

Northrop Grumman, the military and aerospace company, is trying to fight the trend. The company, no longer the Island's largest employer but still an important presence with 2,000 workers in Bethpage, says it has little trouble attracting entry-level engineers with starting salaries of about $55,000 a year. A survey found that nearly one-quarter of its work force, including many of the new employees, lived with parents.

But retaining them is another matter. ''Local schools are turning out good people right out of college, but the problem is keeping them,'' said Tom Sullivan, a senior real estate project manager at Northrop Grumman. ''When the folks who were living with their moms and dads started looking for their homes, that's when the sticker shock hit them.''

The attrition is a financial blow to Northrop Grumman, which spends about $250,000 in salary, benefits, training and other support costs on each engineer over the first three years on the job.

To slow the brain drain, Northrop Grumman started working with two nonprofit groups, the Long Island Housing Partnership and the Community Development Corporation of Long Island, to make it easier for employees to qualify for help putting together a down payment on a new house.

The program has slowed the departures. But Northrop Grumman says it is difficult to attract experienced employees from other parts of the country because of high home prices and taxes.

Workers at Northrop Grumman are not alone. George Ciresi III, an East Patchogue native, returned to the Island in 2003 after spending seven years in college and graduate school in Connecticut. As a physical therapist, he earned $60,000, which he thought would be enough to cover a mortgage. But he couldn't find anything affordable within reasonable commuting distance of his clinic in Great Neck.

''When I was in school, I thought I could afford a house, but the reality of how much it costs has set in,'' said Mr. Ciresi, now 26.

Like a growing number of young professionals on Long Island, Mr. Ciresi moved back in with his parents instead, so he could pay off his student loans and save money for a down payment.

Two years later, his annual income was up to nearly $90,000, including bonuses. But that made him ineligible for many public subsidies and low-cost mortgages intended to help first-time home buyers. Eventually, he found the Community Development Corporation, which helped him with the mortgage on the $270,000 condominium he recently bought in Medford.

Even so, ''I'm just barely able to afford the apartment,'' Mr. Ciresi said. ''I'm very family-oriented and I don't want to leave Long Island. But if it came to living at home for another five years, I might leave.''

Other suburban counties across the country, including those near San Francisco, Washington and Atlanta, suffer from many of the same ills that plague Nassau and Suffolk.

But Long Island's problems are more pronounced than most, because developable land is more scarce and the geography of the Island more constricting: There is hardly a practical way to commute to the Island from some other suburban or rural area, or even most of New York City.

The Island's infrastructure -- its highways, schools, hospitals and water mains -- is also older on average than that of most other suburbs, and more expensive to maintain for that reason as well. And the Island's complex web of local governments and taxing jurisdictions has not made it any easier. Nassau County alone has 3 townships and 2 cities, 64 villages, 58 school districts, 71 fire districts, 24 garbage districts and 36 water districts, among scores of others.

These overlapping layers of local government developed in large part because Long Islanders wanted to exercise local control over the places where they live. But experts point to the inherent duplication and inefficiency of the system, which many call an expensive anachronism.

Anachronistic is also what many advocates call the Island's zoning and land-use laws, which have been shaped by the political imperative to protect homeowners' property values and have arguably worked too well. Now, many housing advocates say, they stand in the way of creative approaches to the housing problem, like higher-density housing and mixed-use developments.

House prices are now so high that residential development tends to crowd out all other land uses on the Island. Brownfield sites that once held job-creating businesses and institutions, like the old Thypin Steel works in Port Washington or the state psychiatric hospitals in Kings Park and Brentwood, are proposed for redevelopment as housing.

Sites like these often need significant environmental cleanup before homes can be built. To pay for that, developers demand permission to build at high densities, or government agencies demand that subsidized housing for low-income people be included in the project -- either of which is likely to provoke major opposition in the community.

Yet without housing that first-time buyers and lower-income people can afford, either on their own or with subsidies, Long Island risks driving away the workers who are needed to keep the local economy running, and the families who are needed to refresh and sustain community life.

''People are starting to realize that there has to be a sea change in attitudes,'' said Jim Morgo, the Suffolk County commissioner of economic development and work-force housing. ''If there isn't affordable housing for our young people, it's a prescription for economic disaster.''
